```
Das wrote:
> Hello, I have a quick trio of COBOL assignments that I need done
> ASAP!!! Please contact me for details, but this will not take an
> experience programmer more than and hour or two!!

This is a really good way to get through life.

Pay other people to do the things you COULD do yourself, if you applied 
yourself and learned to organize your time.

Work and effort are for idiots, not for smart people like you.

The people here all paid other people to do their assignments, too. (We are 
a pretty smart bunch...)  Sadly, that means that nobody here actually KNOWS 
anything and your job would just be sub-contracted to someone in Asia who 
has no option but to actually do the ...(ugh!) work.

Paying someone else to accept YOUR responsibility is a clever move.

It leaves you plenty of time for all the really important things like sex 
and drugs and Rock 'n' Roll and it also ensures that you never develop any 
backbone, integrity, or character, so you will never be troubled by a guilty 
conscience.

The only drawback is that you have to keep paying people and that requires 
cash.

If you have sucker parents that shouldn't be a problem; after all, you 
didn't ask to be born and the fact that they worked hard all their lives 
just shows you are smarter than them. If you don't have parents with money, 
you may need to consider high risk activities like selling drugs or bank 
robbery.Sooner or later this results in tears before bedtime.

Good luck with your future.

(I have a feeling it may not be a long one...)

Pete.
```

- **Date:** 2011-07-03T16:13:33+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<97a8jfF30bU1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<adf436ba-28c8-404f-9e07-cfb5d974690a@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <4qpr0797f3jogaq53cj2c1c0dgrq2mpp31@4ax.com> <20a93b86-e277-40b4-bb5a-c6e7307e7a98@l18g2000yql.googlegroups.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jul 1, 4:27 pm, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
>> On Thu, 30 Jun 2011 14:02:43 -0700 (PDT), Das
…[28 more quoted lines elided]…
> prior to being fast-tracked to the corner office.

Exactly!
>
> I'm really disappointed that Pete let the cat out of the bag. However,
> it was really obvious that we know less about COBOL and more about
> global warming and God than was wise for us.

Obviously, it is not for me to judge the actions of others. But it seems 
ironic that people who have been on record here in the past, complaining 
about the actions of their managers, seem happy to support something that 
can only lead to incompetence in the workplace, never mind the moral and 
human questions involved, once some dollar signs are flashed to them.

"For what shall it profit a man, if he gain the whole world and lose his 
soul?"

Like most people, I am feeling the crunch at the moment.

So I thought a bit more about my position on this.

"At what point would I write the requested code?"  $1000?  $10,000? 
$100,000?

Nope. It just doesn't work for me. I can make a living without doing the 
damage that this causes. And I don't see money as the only purpose in life. 
Sure, it is important, but it ISN'T as important as encouraging a young 
person's progress down a diminishing spiral, or as important as going 
against every principle that allows me to use the adjective "professional" 
when referring to my IT career. Neither does love of money override my 
desire to sleep peacefully at night.

I have been on the bones of my arse during my life. I know what hunger is, I 
know how hard life can be. But there is kindness and decency in human beings 
and even as a stranger in strange lands I have been amazed at the basic 
goodness in people. Life is worth the effort and we have no right to 
knowingly contribute to lessening someone else's experience of it, just to 
indulge our own greed goblins.

If I had a child who was dying, and doing this could pay for an operation? 
What then? (Sure, it is a whimsy, but sometimes examining moral dilemmas can 
reveal things about yourself that you were not aware of.)

Yes, I'd write the code.

Life trumps money every time.

It isn't about adopting the moral high ground and pompously condemning those 
who don't; it is about doing the best you can for all concerned.

I'd do everything I could to persuade the OP that this is not a good course 
of action to habitually pursue, and I'd try and get him to see he is worth 
more than that.

But I'd write the code.

However, I don't have a dying child (or even a dying dog or cat) and there 
is still food in the fridge and  a roof over my head, so it isn't hard to 
decide. I'll take a swerve on the chance to make a bit on the side, if not 
illegally, at least immorally.

Of course, those of you who don't take such a swerve will never post again 
about poor management, right?

Pete.
(Who sleeps like a baby)
```

Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> On Jul 1, 4:27 pm, SkippyPB <swieg...@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote:
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
>>>

[snip]

>"For what shall it profit a man, if he gain the whole world and lose his 
>soul?"

>"At what point would I write the requested code?"  $1000?  $10,000? 
>$100,000?

The old-fashioned Usual Terms can be tried for starters... half up front, 
half on completion.

(the usual guarantee of second-half payment consists of a subroutine which 
checks a clock and either works or doesn't)

[snip]

>And I don't see money as the only purpose in life. 

Someone notify Reuters, we are in agreement.  During a conference call on 
Friday I behaved in a manner which some might describe as being 'not the 
most professional'... and others as 'losing it, big-time'.  This means it 
it is time for... A Story.

Friday afternoon conference call, usually it is idiot schedules such 
things.  We've got one scheduled every Friday, 1530 - 1700.  Anyhow, there 
are several groups involved and everyone is busy whining and pointing 
fingers... and I snapped.

The project I'm on lost all budget in October, 2010, and started up again 
with a newer, smaller staff in March 2011.  Since then there has been 
nothing but nit-picking, blame, strife and tediousness... today's example 
was of someone, I know not who, blaming my group for holding up progress 
because everything was Working Just Dandy back in 2010 and was Almost 
Ready for Production Implementation and then there was just this Slight 
Pause and this New Group (mine) came on-board and nothing's been able to 
do what he'd seen it do before...

... and I snapped.  'Mr Doakes,' I asked, 'when did you see the system 
demonstrated?'

'Well, *I* personally didn't see it but I was told by Mr Oakes that 
everything was in order...'

'Yes, I see.  When did Mr Oakes leave for a new project, Mr Doakes?'

'That must have been about... November or so.'

'Very good.  Now, Mr Doakes, I will not question what you told me... but 
you did *not* tell me the system worked, you said Mr Oakes told you he saw 
it work.  Mr Oakes is not present so the best evidence you can give us 
about this system is hearsay, based from November of last year.  I don't 
know what configuration changes have been made, I *do* know that new 
hardware and software have been installed and I know that I've followed 
the documentation I've been given, step by step, time and time again, and 
not gotten the results that are expected.  Something here is not right so 
I'll make you and offer:

'Come over to my cubicle, Mr Doakes.  Use the equipment assigned to me, 
full access to the email communications I have received and equally full 
access to the documentation I have.

'According to your evaluation this should be enough to accomplish a 
certain task.

'According to *my* experience it isn't... and I'm willing to bet one 
paycheck, yours against mine, on that outcome.  Money talks, Mr Doakes, 
and... something else rhymes.'

The room went dead quiet... remember, I have no idea with whom I am am 
speaking or who just heard the 'put up or shut up' I had just issued.  My 
tech lead turned a shade of red I haven't seen since my last days as a 
volunteer in the Ememergency Room of St Shapiro's Hospital and said 'All 
right, I'm putting an end to this thread right now...'

... and I told him 'It would be impolite to allow you to interrupt me so 
impolitely, Mr Jones, so let *me* finish by saying that I'm just... tired 
of all this.  I'm tired.  Requests get made, requests get changed, fingers 
get pointed, this happens all the time.  What *else* happens, Mr Jones, is 
that I have *never* seen you encourage, support or take the side of one of 
your subordinates.  Never.

'Leaving aside how questionable a tactic this is - we are here to make you 
look *good*, Mr Jones, and if you think that degrading our skills 
encourages us to do so then our experiences are different - we are here to 
work *with* you.  What kind of thinking leads to the conclusion of 'I have 
a group of intelligent, technichnally competent, *sharp* people with whom 
I'm supposed to work to complete a project... so the best way to 
accomplish that goal is to spite, degrade and second-guess each and every 
word that comes out of their mouths?''

'On second thought, Mr Jones, don't tell me how you reached this 
conclusion... I've opened my mouth and spoken what I see as truth, without 
language that could not be printed on the front page of a family newspaper 
and with an intention of making things better for everyone involved on 
this project.

*Nobody* that I know of looks forward to coming to work for this job.  The 
work is fascinating, we are doing something that's never been accomplished 
on this sort of scale before, the employees like the kind of work they've 
chosen to make their livings... and nobody likes how this working out.

I've said too much.  It is Friday, a hair after four pm, and I am going 
home.  I'll see you all come Tuesday.

Should be interesting to see if my building-access ID Card still works, 
eh?

DD
```

"Idiots Management Handbook", again... Encourage team members to whine and

Idiots Management Handbook...  "It worked before you got your hands on it." 
(Ploy 732 for deflecting attention from what needs fixing.)

I had a man in Australia say that to me once and I left the room. Had I not 
done so I would have undoubtedly hit him. All work immediately ceased on the 
project. A week later a neutral third party asked if I would be prepared to 
talk about it. I said: "No." Half-an-hour later phone rang again with an 
apology relayed through the third party. I agreed that was a step in the 
right direction and went back. After explaining to the idiot why I was 
offended (he had no idea...), we got down to examining the real issues and 
it was a successful venture. I understand how mortifying it is when someone 
who really has no idea of what you actually deal with, the effort you and 
your colleagues have invested and the constant hurdles placed in the way by 
people like him,  resorts to a simplistic statement like "it used to work". 
No, it didn't. If it did, there would be no reason to change it. It NEVER 
worked previously in the way it is now required to because you wouldn't be 
implementing changes if it did.

> ... and I snapped.  'Mr Doakes,' I asked, 'when did you see the system

…[28 more quoted lines elided]…

"Dashwood's Manual for Successful Management of IT Projects"... a key 
indicator of team morale is how they feel about coming to work. If they hate 
it, you have failed. It IS your problem if they are miserable and see the 
workplace as a frustrating grind with no fun or joy of achievement.

remote vestige of any proper management practice in the group who were in 
the conference call, you may find a meeting when you return that is prepared 
to address the issues you raised.

If they are really just useless COIs they will close ranks, label you a 
"troublemaker" (or, my personal favourite: "A non-team player"...he plays 
for a "non-team"?, just the skills we need... :-)), and proceed to get rid

respect the fact that you spoke your mind, see there are some serious issues 
here and try to get all parties round a table, without heat, to look at what 
can be done to improve things. (Probably get Mr.Jones on a management 
seminar or at least some basic training in dealing with people.)

Boss: "So this Dwarf guy is a bit of a problem for you?"

Jones: "No Sir. He is just anti-authority and undermines whatever I try and 
do. I try to get your policies implemented but this guy just stirs 
resentment. He is difficult to work with ... yada yada yada"  (This is an 
expected spiteful reaction because you undressed him in public...)

The fact is that the project is not progressing. The Boss (if he's any good) 
will realize that Jones is way too concerned with arse-licking than he is 
with the building of a coherent and capable team and he'll address that.

If he isn't any good he'll support Jones and your contract will not be 
renewed. If they can find the slightest cause they will terminate you and in 
the meantime your life will not be easy.

While all of the above is pure speculation on my part, as you are aware, I 
am not exactly a stranger to these situations so I justify my comments by 
saying they come from an informed base of experience, over 40 years, on both 
sides of the fence. :-)

I remember many years ago escorting a young Australian lady for a weekend in 
Rotorua, which is a popular scenic resort in the Bay of Plenty (not on the 
coast).

We were cruising the main drag searching through the dozens of motels for 
something that might be OK. (Actually, these days they are all of a very 
high standard, but this was long ago.) We finally found one and inspected 
the grounds and facilities, including the souvenir shop.

I asked her what she thought and she said, a bit wistfully: "They haven't 
got a pearl."

I guessed she wanted this oyster product as a souvenir for herself or a 
family member and suggested we repair to some of the excellent jewellery 
shops in the city.

"No, silly, they haven't got a swemming pearl!" (It translates as "swimming 
pool")

(We did end up in a motel with a "swemming pearl" but we never got around to 
using it... :-))

few of them in from the Southern Ocean) and I was considering the delightful 
image of her "seally beach", when I suddenly realized she was describing a 
girl she knew who had done something foolish. ("silly bitch").

English is like a foreign language to many nationalities that speak it.

```
In article <97m2olFde2U1@mid.individual.net>,
Pete Dashwood <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
>docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
>> In article <31i8171ce41a6qbs4qfsji6iq2bkan4lai@4ax.com>,
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
>comment was true and fair, but you probably shouldn't have made it publicly.

E'en better, Mr Dashwood... sometimes The Universe just falls into place.  
My episode was on Monday; on Tuesday my quiet, shy, modest and retiring 
wife drove me into the shop (consultants can't be trusted with external 
access, you know) and waited what she felt was too long.  When Mr Jones 
passed by she recognised him and told him to get my anatomy out, pronto.  
Mr Jones put his hand on my shoulder and said 'You'd rather be in *here* 
than at home with *her*?  You really *are* insane, man, now get out before 
you lose it completely.'

The next day I stumped into Mr Jones' office - he was talking with someone 
but I kind of... moved with that 'this is an old guy with a cane and you 
don't get into his way because... well, because nobody ever gets into the 
way of an old guy with a cane like this and comes off the better for 
it'... and I pointed my cane at the lad and said 'I have no idea who you 
are and I might be putting my career in danger by addressing you in this 
manner but my wife has told *me* to pass a message along to Mr Jones... 
and I *live* with my wife.'

I then said 'Mr Jones, this is more-or-less verbatim... 'Tell Mr Jones 
that he is a real dolly for getting you out of there so fast yesterday and 
being so considerate of your condition.'

Mr Jones smiled and said 'And *you* tell *your* wife that *she* is a dolly 
and a sweetheart, to boot...' and I interrupted with 'Mr Jones, she is my 
wife and I know her... if she was any more of a dolly she'd be a 
fork-lift'

(I will not explain the joke... but it is one about equipment used in 
mechanics' shops and the like and was so unexpected that Mr Jones said 
'Look at this guy... his brain's half blown out from seizures and he's 
*still* thinking in jokes I couldn't come up with in a hundred years...'

Mr Jones then told me about a fellow he worked with who had epilepsy and 
changed meds to cut down on his weight gain... and threw a grand mal 
seizure in the shop.  He said the best he's seen me do is slur my speech a 
bit and I got a high standard to beat.

Humor helps... and it can even be funny, too.

[snip]

>If the "medical difficulty" is with you, best wishes for a speedy recovery.

I threw a seizure (out of the blue, no history, no seeming action of 
stimulus) so they started me on levetiracetam (Keppra), 500mg, once a day 
for one week, upped to twice a day (morn and eve) for the next week, 
shifted to one in the morn and a Divalproex, 500mg in the evening, for a 
week and then Divalproex, 500mg, bid (morn and eve)... and at the end of a 
week of *that* I threw another seizure.  It was July 4th and the kind of 
area I inhabit doesn't generate much hospital activity on a holiday so I 
got good, fast service... even an MRI.

Nobody knows what is going on... but my levels of ammonia indicated the 
Divalproex should be stopped, *now*.  That was Tuesday... and I've been 
getting better ever since.

DD
```

The last time I hit a girl, I was 8 years old. She told her father, who told

Universe works in this regard. My Dad was six foot and a professional boxer. 
You tended to listen when he spoke and I remember the lesson to this day. I 
have never hit  a woman since. Neither would I do so. In self-defence, if 
attacked physically by a woman, I would prevent her hurting me, but I would 
not attack her in retaliation.

My ex-wife came from a culture where hitting women was normal (they believed 
that if a man hit you it was because he loved you...) and tried to provoke 
me on a number of occasions. I simply left the house and drove around until 
I was calm. (also not recommended; these days I don't get behind the wheel 
if I am angry or upset...)

I do believe in equality for people (men and women), but that doesn't extend 
to hitting women, even though I would, under extreme provocation, hit a man.

I guess I'm not up with modern Political Correctness in that regard.

Pete.

```
Howard Brazee wrote:
> On Sat, 9 Jul 2011 13:19:41 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[8 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Having a father who was a boxer and receiving my first gloves at age 6, I 
was always into boxing. I enjoyed it and was good at it but I never went 
looking for trouble and I never bullied other kids. (I did stop some bullies 
from torturing a kid who later became one of my best friends and we shared a 
flat in our teens. This was achieved without actual violence but I would 
have used violence had it been necessary.)

I think it is very much as you say. Kids often do what they think they are 
supposed to. I was brought up to believe that the strong should defend the 
weak, and was inspired by stories like Arthur and Robin Hood. I can see now 
that the heroic role models I found in literature as a kid definitely shaped 
my attitude into later life. I've always had a thing about fair play and am 
passionate about it. This is also a part of the local culture in NZ where we 
expect people to get a "fair go".

As for the desire to hit people, I think it stems from frustration that 
there sometimes seems to be no other way to get a hearing. As I mentioned 
elsewhere, these days I just leave the room when I feel this urge arising. 
(And it is also very unusual for me to reach that stage). Usually it doesn't 
take long to recover equilibrium. There definitely are more productive ways 
to settle disputes than with physical violence, but sometimes anger and 
frustration can throw reason out the window.

Doc's example of the horrid little girl who DESERVES a smack in the chops, 
is a good one. Most of us have encountered such children. (or were such... 
:-))  I remember when I first embarked on a teaching career and it was still 
usual to beat children with canes and straps, a lecturer saying to me: "If 
that kid you are about to beat with a cane was the same size and weight as 
you, and also had a cane, would you still try and flog him?" It was the most 
potent argument I ever heard for NOT beating children and I'm pleased to say 
that in the short time I was actually a teacher, I never used corporal 
punishment on any student. (And I was teaching at a very frightening school 
in the worst suburb of South Auckland.)

So, armed with life experience, I have to say, I wouldn't smack Doc's 
example kid, even given the provocation he described. I'd remind myself I am 
many times bigger and harder and have nothing to prove to a kid. My response 
would be: "Yes, you are right. Real Men don't beat little girls. But we do 
deny them outings and ice cream and might even violate their Human Rights by 
taking their cell phones off them for a while, until they learn some 
manners." Smile sweetly and pick up her phone. (or similar... I'm sure you 
catch my drift :-))




> What I object to is people who think anger is an excuse for violent
> action, as there are people who get angry (or drunk) to allow them to
> act violently.    We are in more danger of people who kill out of rage
> than we are out of people who kill in cold blood for the insurance.

I agree.

Some people (usually the ones who are not the sharpest tool in the box) feel 
that if they are not responsible (drunk, angry, emotional) and not in 
control then they are not accountable for what they do and so violence is 
OK.

These are the truly dangerous.

There is nothing in our world which poses such a threat as stupidity does.

Pete.
```

```
In article <hfef17ptmjs74vinr1t91f8i0fmudqgb11@4ax.com>,
Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>On Sat, 9 Jul 2011 13:19:41 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
><dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
>than we are out of people who kill in cold blood for the insurance.

And we (as residents of the United States of America) are more in danger 
from ourselves than from others; according to 
http://www.cdc.gov/NCHS/data/nvsr/nvsr58/nvsr58_19.pdf the 11th-highest 
cause of death is 'self-inflicted harm', 12th is chronic liver disease and 
cirrhosis (usually, but not always, alcohol-related)... and 15th is 
assault (homicide).

On the bright side... the really high numbers are more age-related 
disorders (heart disease, malignant neoplasms, Alzheimers).  If one is 
struck with nostalgia for the Goode Olde Dayse it is instructive to 
compare causes of death a century back to the current set, toss in the 
average longevity of a child who lives past 60 months and see what 
conclusions can be teased out.

DD
```

It's an interesting idea, but not borne out in our family. I never saw my

from an altercation. Fortunately these were extremely infrequent.


>

You'd lose.

My mother-in-law (who I was very fond of) brought up 5 children, with a 
husband who was away most of the time (he was an Engineering officer in the 
Merchant Marine). When he was home he was mostly drunk, but apparently he 
never hit her or any of the kids. Quite exceptional for the community they 
lived in. When she died, her (grown up) son committed suicide. It was a 
tragic family in many ways.

> New rule to live by (learned just this past week)
>
> No civilization can survive without traditions.

Tradition is great for the security and stability it brings. The trick is 
knowing when to start a new tradition and when to let go of some of the 
older ones. :-)

> No civilization can grow without destroying those same traditions.

Tradition represents resistance to change and no progress is possible 
without change. (Not all change represents progress, of course...) So I'd 
agree that your statement is mostly true. (With the more harmless 
traditions, there will be little effect on the growth of society whether 
they are observed or not.)

Some interesting ideas; thanks for your post.

Pete.

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Quick COBOL Assignment Needing Done ASAP!! $$$

_(thread depth: 11)_

- **From:** "Pete Dashwood" <dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz>
- **Date:** 2011-07-12T14:10:42+12:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<981op4Fcf4U1@mid.individual.net>`
- **References:** `<adf436ba-28c8-404f-9e07-cfb5d974690a@gh5g2000vbb.googlegroups.com> <iv5mqh$gid$2@reader1.panix.com> <97oddfFf6uU1@mid.individual.net> <-PedndqnkdLLjIbTnZ2dnUVZ_gednZ2d@earthlink.com> <ivg5p5$lve$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```
docdwarf@panix.com wrote:
> In article <-PedndqnkdLLjIbTnZ2dnUVZ_gednZ2d@earthlink.com>,
> HeyBub <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> different civilization?

Self examination is far from impossible, Doc.

Some would argue that it is essential for a successful civilization to keep 
an eye on itself in terms of quantifiable things and to stimulate debates 
like this one on things which cannot be quantified (and therefore reside in 
the domain of opinion).

I think it is a good idea to monitor personal growth as well. About once a 
year I like to reflect on how I feel about life now as opposed to how I felt 
last year :-) Usually I can see where lessons were learned (and try to make 
sure that it wasn't the wrong lesson), and I can see areas that need work 
and areas where progress was made. Age is a great mellowing influence and 
also helps with perspective. I can't prove it, but I would imagine that 
civilizations which last for a very long time are probably getting it mostly 
right. Then some completely unexpected event occurs (or they become so 
accepting of their own success that they stop monitoring themselves) and the 
civilization falls.

It is unthinkable that the USA could run out of money, or that nobody would 
be prepared to lend to them.

Yet there is a small but finite chance that it could happen.

It is hard for outsiders like me to see how much of this is political 
posturing (like Republicans forcing the Government to admit that they can't 
manage) and how much of it is actually real. Either way, I should think that 
many Americans are considering and reviewing their options right now.

They showed Al Gore's "An Inconvenient Truth" here on TV a few nights ago 
and I found it very interesting. I hadn't seen it and it is a bit dated now, 
but the facts are pretty much inescapable. America consumes most of the 
worlds resources and much of that consumption is not necessary in order for 
people to have a reasonable standard of living. Of course, many Americans 
are concerned about it, but it is hard to see how much progress is being 
made towards less waste and better utilization.

I can remember when the NZ economy rested on sheep. (We had around 60 
million of them at a time when the population was under three million; if 
the sheep had ever become organised it could have been decidedly unpleasant 
for us...). At that time it was just assumed that the wool cheques would 
keep rolling in and could prop up our welfare state indefinitely. We sent 
lamb and wool to England and they sent large sums of money back. Then Ted 
Heath became enamoured with the European Union and had visions of everyone 
in Europe driving mini-minors and the sun never setting on a bright 
industrial future for the UK, so off they went. (Heath's dream didn't 
eventuate; everyone bought volkswagens because they were better cars and it 
was Germany that did so well they were able to buy the Eastern bloc back and 
get reunited...)

It seemed that we were heading for bankruptcy on the withdrawal of support 
from the Motherland. Then people kind of woke up and scratched their arses 
and started thinking there was a lot more we could do besides raising sheep 
for dear old Blighty. Today, we are a much more diverse nation with twice 
the population and many more important "industries", although agriculture 
and farming are still extremely important for us. The economy is supposed to 
be knowledge based (although judging by some of the experiences I've had in 
certain local establishments, I think there is still work required in that 
area) and we send our young people to Academic institutions where they get 
saddled with huge debts that require them to leave the country so they can 
make "loadsa money" in real foreign currencies and pay off their student 
loans. Most of them eventually come back (a bit like me, really...) and 
bring their assets with them so it is all good in the long run.

Although life here is far from perfect financially (like most nations we are 
incurring debt at an alarming rate, but the lenders seem to think we are 
good for it and investment is coming in - the Chinese decided to invest 6 
billion dollars in NZ a few weeks ago; I might have to start learning 
Mandarin...), the sudden pulling of the fiscal rug back in the 60s was 
actually a Very Good Thing. It made us get into other things and learn not 
to put all the eggs in one basket.

Maybe the current situation in the USA will jolt the population in a similar 
way, and in 10 years time, when everyone is driving hybrids and using 
renewable energy, when the US has signed up to Kyoto, when people will pay a 
little more to have things produced responsibly, everyone will look back on 
this time as a needed stimulus which producd an overall good result.

Of course, by then I'll be living on Chow Mein and watching Kung Fu 
movies...

Pete.
```

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jul 12, 1:36 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
>> Pete Dashwood wrote:
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> so you can expect to run the aircon for a few centuries.

I'm in Houston. We didn't build the first air-conditioned sports stadium to 
be ostentatious - air conditioning was ALREADY required! And that was back 
in the '60's.

What global warming? Some say world-wide temperatures have stabilized - or 
even declined slightly - since 1998. Others say Hell is coming to us. It's 
hard to decide when the interpretation of the evidence is so varied.

My beef with global warming is the fear-mongers. Assuming, arguendo, that 
the planet really IS warming, will that be so bad? There are fewer deaths 
associated with warm than with cold. Many places, such as Canada, will be 
able to harvest three crops per year instead of two, the Northwest Passage 
may be a year-round thing, much to the benefit of both China and Europe, and 
other beneficial results will accrue.

Conversely, the effects of this supposed warming can probably be dealt with 
more cheaply than an effort to prevent the warming in the first place, 
assuming, again arguendo, that it CAN be dealt with.

No, the whole business is unsettled.

HeyBub wrote:
> Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> On Jul 12, 1:36 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
…[35 more quoted lines elided]…
> No, the whole business is unsettled.

I agree completely that it isn't clear.

But the question you have to ask is: Can we take the risk and continue on 
our merry way, then suffer disatrous consequences if the people who support 
the Global Warming argument turn out to be right?

Despite the advantages you mention, Jerry, millions of people will be 
homeless or underwater if the ice caps melt as predicted. Maybe, if the idea 
of conservation is abhorrent on the grounds of appeasing Greenies, it should 
be considered as desirable because of the money it saves...:-)
 (I made sure the new appliances I bought are highly energy efficient and I 
am looking at making the whole house more so. I don't know if it will save 
the planet, but it will certainly save me money. :-))

If you look at the amount of energy that falls on every square mere of the 
Earth, and how much of that we currently harness (less than 1%) there is 
more than enough energy to meet all the foreseeable needs (apart from 
esoteric ideas like creating a local wormhole, which would take more energy 
than is available in our solar system), so the problem really is just to 
find better ways to harness it.

Oddly enough, I have faith in the ingenuity of Mankind and I believe this 
problem will be solved, just as the problem of feeding people has been 
largely solved (at least in advanced societies). I remember dire predictions 
in the 60s that we would be incapable of producing enough food to support a 
world population of 3 billion. We now have twice that number, and while far 
too many ppeople are hungry in the developing world, that is being addressed 
and the developed world is suffering no shortage.

We need challenges to help us grow.

Pete.

sufficient to feed 12 billion (just under double the number that
currently inhabit our planet). In the 40 years that I have been aware
of global problems with food production, water provision, pollution
levels, war, overpopulation (and any other green issue you care to
mention) not one of the problems has been resolved. The Horn of Africa
is still suffering from famines, droughts, disease and war just as it
did 40 years ago. Global warming is not going to improve the
conditions in that region.

AND, even in the UK we have sections of society who live below the
poverty line and others who can afford central heating OR food in
winter but NOT both.

As they say in some African countries: Plus ca change, plus la meme
chose. (accents omitted).
```

Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jul 13, 1:00 am, "Pete Dashwood"
> <dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:
…[12 more quoted lines elided]…
> efficient than it will save in energy costs.

It depends over what period you are considering it. I am satisfied that 
within 2 to 3 years everything I'm doing will pay for itself and after that 
it is a saving.

Insulation is an obvious one and the Government here gives a grant for it. 
I've already done that. I'm considering solar water heating (Tauranga has a 
sub-tropical climate and we get plenty of sunshine), but for the moment, I'm 
going with switching off the hot water tank (a very large energy consumer) 
and going with instant water heating via Triton devices. The shower I 
installed cost $600 and the running cost is 18 cents for 10 minutes. It will 
pay for itself in less than a year. The solar heating is more expensive but 
hot water costs are also expensive. The dishwasher heats its own water, the 
washing machine uses cold and if I actually need hot water for any other 
purpose (and nothing comes to mind) I can heat it as required.

I may install a heat pump but that also is not cheap. (Several thousand 
dollars) However, they are highly efficient and the savings are significant. 
Probably a 3 to 5 year investment for that one.

All of this assumes the cost of electricity (currently around 35 cents per 
KWh) does not increase. If it does, then the ROI is achieved in a shorter 
time frame.


>
>>
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> production.

Last time I looked, the sun was not running on coal... :-)

Fossil fuels are not a good bet for the energy future, even if there are 
still untapped deposits of them. Personally, I think nuclear energy is the 
answer, but I agree there are risks associated with it (at least with the 
current level of breeder reactor design, although this too is evolving and 
improving - a modern nuclear plant is a long way from Calder Hall...). The 
next best thing would be to make better use of sunlight, but it remains to 
be seen how we might best go about that. Hydrogen has to be a good fuel for 
the future as we have almost unlimited amounts of it in the oceans, and when 
it burns it creates water. It isn't just "all ya gotta do"; there are 
difficult and real challenges involved in any of these solutions but I 
believe those challenges will be met. That was my point. Not that humans are 
too greedy or political to implement worthwhile solutions, but that humans 
have the ingenuity to solve these problems at least at a technology level.

>>
>> Oddly enough, I have faith in the ingenuity of Mankind and I believe
…[14 more quoted lines elided]…
> mention) not one of the problems has been resolved.

If someone develops a vaccine for a certain disease but the people who need 
it refuse to take it, is the problem solved?

(It is certainly solved technically, even if it isn't solved politically...)

I was speaking in terms of the technology. The technology exists to solve 
all of these problems. Getting them applied is a different set of problems 
which has nothing to do with Global Warming. If certain countries prefer to 
buy tanks and warplanes rather than trucks to distribute food to their 
population, that is not a technology problem.

>The Horn of Africa
> is still suffering from famines, droughts, disease and war just as it
> did 40 years ago. Global warming is not going to improve the
> conditions in that region.

Neither will it make them any worse. Those are not problems that require 
ingenuity to resolve; they are political problems which have to do with a 
culture and Education system (or lack of it).

>
> AND, even in the UK we have sections of society who live below the
> poverty line and others who can afford central heating OR food in
> winter but NOT both.

Again, those are social, not technology problems.

>
> As they say in some African countries: Plus ca change, plus la meme
> chose. (accents omitted).

Yes, but it isn't strictly true. There is progress. There are technologies 
that have enabled the eradication of certain diseases and the production of 
food, that couldn't be imagined 50 years ago. What is "plus la meme chose" 
is human culture and politics. However,  it looks as if there is a sea 
change even in that at the moment. More and more people want to cast of 
oppressive dictatorships and have a say in their own destiny. If they can 
change the administration, they can change the administration's policies.

We live in an interesting time.

Pete.

> If you look at the amount of energy that falls on every square mere
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> so the problem really is just to find better ways to harness it.

Nonsense. There's about 1300 watts per sq meter of solar radiation falling 
on the earth. At the equator. At noon. With no clouds. Assuming solar 
collectors with a 50% efficiency rating, and adjusting for darkness, 
latitude, clouds, and, say, 10% redundancy, it would take a solar collector 
farm the size of the Los Angeles basin (~1200 sq miles) to provide enough 
power for just California (~50GW).

Now compute the cost to build and maintain 1,200 square miles of ANYTHING, 
let alone solar collectors. The only good that can be said about the scheme 
is that the residents of Los Angeles, living under the thing, would be in 
the dark.

Even if you somehow succeeded with harnessing the sun, you haven't really 
addressed the energy used by transportation.

It is physically and economically impossible to run a city, province, 
country, hemisphere off of sunbeams. Even if you threw in moonbeams and

has a McDonald's).

```
HeyBub wrote:
> Pete Dashwood wrote:
>> HeyBub wrote:
…[74 more quoted lines elided]…
> Nonsense.

No more nonsense than Democracy and famine... see below.


> There's about 1300 watts per sq meter of solar radiation
> falling on the earth.

That is a HUGE amount of energy. (I have figures that differ from that but 
the actual amount isn't really important as long as we agree it is "large".)


>At the equator. At noon. With no clouds.

So, why can't we collect it above the atmosphere?

Just because we haven't done so doesn't mean we can't.

The communications satellites we now take for granted were predicted many 
years before anyone got round to building them. When the need bcame apparent 
the technology responded.

> Assuming solar collectors with a 50% efficiency rating, and adjusting
> for darkness, latitude, clouds, and, say, 10% redundancy, it would
> take a solar collector farm the size of the Los Angeles basin (~1200
> sq miles) to provide enough power for just California (~50GW).

But your assumption is flawed. You are basing it on a surface based 
implementation using current technology. Your assumption does not account 
for research currently under way to improve the efficiency of solar 
collectors and it doesn't even consider the possibility of simply reflecting 
sunlight directly to the surface by focussed mirrors, for example.  (A bunch 
of these could be spread around the planet so that some of them were always 
available, just like comm satellites are.) A receptor on an ocean platform 
could transfer the energy into a form we could use. And it doesn't HAVE to 
be electicity derived from solar panels. The energy could be used to 
electrolyse sea water and provide hydrogen as a fuel, for example. It would 
be a bit like having "hydrogen platforms" much as we have "oil platforms" at 
the moment.) Right now, we don't have an adequate way to transfer energy 
without wires (microwaves?) but that isn't something that should be beyond 
us. The point is that until humans apply themselves to these questions they 
won't be solved. I don't know whether any of the ideas noted are viable 
because I'm not an engineer, but I bet if you got a bunch of engineers and 
scientists they could come up with something. It is NOT about saying "We 
can't do that because our current technology can't do it." It is about 
pushing the extent of current technology and applying science and 
imagination.

>
> Now compute the cost to build and maintain 1,200 square miles of
> ANYTHING, let alone solar collectors. The only good that can be said
> about the scheme is that the residents of Los Angeles, living under
> the thing, would be in the dark.

:-)

>
> Even if you somehow succeeded with harnessing the sun, you haven't
> really addressed the energy used by transportation.

But energy can be converted and once you get over the idea of running 
everything on fossil fuels, new possibilities emerge. You certainly can't do 
anything if there is NO energy source to start with, but you can if you have 
a boundless source. The problem then is one of converting and harnessing, 
not generating, energy (which the laws of Physics prevent anyway, except 
under very special circumstances.)

>
> It is physically and economically impossible to run a city, province,
> country, hemisphere off of sunbeams.

It certainly is if you don't have the will to do it.



> Even if you threw in moonbeams
> and pixie dust.
>

They are not required... sunlight can do it easily :-)

>>
>> Oddly enough, I have faith in the ingenuity of Mankind and I believe
…[9 more quoted lines elided]…
> a political problem, not a resource one.

Sorry, I never heard of Paul Ehrlich. If he reached the same conclusion as 
me he is obviously a sagacious and noble fellow... :-)
>
> There has never been a famine in a democracy (or, possibly, a country
> that has a McDonald's).

Not true. Even Amartya Sen who originated this idea agreed that there were 
famines in India after they achieved self rule. (Bihar province is the 
famous one)

Sen wrote his Nobel winning paper in 1999, but in 2001 - 2003, Malawi, which 
became a democracy in 1994, suffered  severe famines. OK, there is currently 
no McDonald's franchise in Malawi but there WAS, briefly, and you might 
smile at this:
http://tachenysinmalawi.blogspot.com/2009/04/is-it-mcdonalds.html

Sen's idea was that a free press and political opposition could prevent 
famine caused by administrative mismanagement , and, at the time he said it, 
it was probably true. Since then there have been a number of examples where 
it didn't work. (See Malawi and Bihar, above)

Athens, the cradle of Democracy, suffered a severe famine in 405 BC. (there 
is no record of whether they had a McDonalds at the time...)

Democracy, in and of itself, does not necessarily avert famine.

McDonalds on the other hand, might be a better bet :-)

Pete

On Jul 15, 3:57 pm, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashw...@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>
> No need for bulldozers and if they are placed in iternational waters the
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> for wires.

Hydrogen diffuses through most materials, including steel and glass.
In doing so it also makes that material (especially steel) brittle,
and thus more likely to crack.

Shipping it in containers over long distances would lead to
significant losses. The 'natural' rate of diffusion for the best
materials is about 1% per day.


> (Sure, there are dangers with transporting liquid hydrogen, but
> there are dangers transporting petroleum too. The advances in materials and
> containment mean that hydrogen is less scary than it was at the time of the
> Hindenberg...)

Most hydrogen airships used 'goldbeaters skin' as being the least
porous flexible material available at the time. It is made from the
gut of calfs.

>imagination.


The problem, as always, is economics.  While collecting ten times more
power per unit collector area in orbit is plausible - just assume some
combination of increased time in the sun, better angle, better cells
(given the cost of getting stuff into orbit, the cost of ultra-high
efficiency cells should be easy to justify), and increased energy flux
from the Sun (the 1300W/sq m is at the top of the atmosphere, at
ground level, it's about 1000W).  It might be a bit more or less (more
likely) than ten times, but in that vicinity somewhere.

And let's completely ignore the transmission from orbit issues.

The problem then is the relative cost of planting 10 square meters of
cells in the local desert (or 20 sq m in a less optimal, but closer,
location), verses putting a square meter of cells in orbit (there is
*nothing* on the horizon that offers a realistic shot at anything less
than a couple of thousand dollars per Kg to LEO).  And then you're
talking about orders of magnitude higher expenses for the orbital
system.

Then the issue is that you can easily build ground based pilot plants,
for very low investments.  And those scale quite predictably and
simply.

So until launch costs come down at *least* a factor of ten from the
best available today, you'll be able to build the equivalent output
array on the ground for far less money.
```

## Thread: Cheap/free compiler — 4 this month / 5 total

_Topics: [`Compilers and vendors`](topics.md#compilers)_

_Thread started [2011-06](2011-06.md#t-4032832e38)._

#### ↳ Cheap/free compiler

- **From:** Nomen Nescio <nobody@dizum.com>
- **Date:** 2011-07-01T17:28:39+02:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<d113564c9487fcb9399b4fd49e1b91b9@dizum.com>`
- **References:** `<4e0cdf10$1@news.x-privat.org>`

```
> I can pick up a smallish COBOL project, but I need a cheap/free compiler.
> Last time I still had access to Micro-Focus, but I don't think it'll still
…[4 more quoted lines elided]…
> Are there any alternatives?

As far as I could tell Micro Focus (Fujitsu?) no longer gives away any free
version, the academic version seems to have been cancelled.

I do not know of any free COBOL for PC except the half-baked OpenCOBOL and
various other so-called "free software" projects. None of those are worth
anything. Pete hasn't chimed in yet and that is a bad sign. He would
probably know.

You can bootleg older versions of Micro Focus or Power COBOL off the net. I
don't know how much paying for a license is or how easy it is to buy
something useful for a private individual but if you can make enough to
cover the cost of a license and still have enough left over after your
project that seems like the honorable thing to do.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: Cheap/free compiler

- **From:** Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.maclean@gmail.com>

I predict that TV and computer will move into each other. You will see TVs 
that handle email, connect to social networks, run GOOGLE searches and, 
eventually, allow you to run remote apps (probably from the cloud) and 
provided by suppliers like GOOGLE and Microsoft, just for openers.

Large, flat screen, TVs with surface technology will become computer 
terminals without you buying add-ons. In effect, your TV will become a large 
iPad... :-)

I don't have cable so I'm not sure what services are available here on it or 
what they cost. I agree that, for the most part, the free-to-air movies are 
crap, but then it is really a matter of opinion...somebody must like them or 
there'd be no point in airing them.

Recently I bought a new digital SLR (Nikon 3100) largely to do an assignment 
for a forthcoming book connected with the Rugby World Cup, which is being 
written by a friend of mine and he asked me to do the photography. Saturday 
morning found me up bright and early (a very rare occurrence; I generally 
don't do mornings... :-)) photographng the nine-year-olds playing Rugby at a 
local park. It was delightful. Early morning Winter sunshine, really hot 
coffee and kids whose enthusiasm and sportsmanship was an example to adults.

(Before getting into computers at age 20, I was a Press photographer with 
the local newspaper here, and I have had a passion for photography all my 
life. Before the digital age, I took slides and processed them in the 
garage...digital is much less messy :-).) Anyway, the new camera takes HD 
movies (limited by what can be stored on the flash card; usually about 10 
minutes) as well as photographs. I couldn't help smiling at how easy it all 
is now. Remove the HDSC card from the camera, insert into laptop, copy to 
backup, clear card, edit and review photos/movies with various photo 
processing software (no, I don't have Adobe Photoshop but I have 3 other 
packages that do everything I need), then connect to large screen TV over 
HDMI interface. Not a single chemical or solution required, no need for a 
thermometer or darkroom and the whole process done in minutes with the 
results viewable on TV... :-)

I never would have thought that TV would figure in my photography, but it 
does.

Who knows what other roles our TV screens will play in the future?

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: TV versus the Computer

- **From:** Richard <riplin@Azonic.co.nz>

```
On Jul 5, 12:55 pm, Richard <rip...@Azonic.co.nz> wrote:

> However, touch screen TVs are not useful. If I am sitting on a couch
> watching TV I do not want to get up to poke a finger on the screen.
> Remotes were developed to avoid that. You can get full keyboard
> 'remotes'. Actually you have been able to since the late 80s, they
> were called Apple II, Commodore 64, etc.

That should have been 'late 70s'. The Apple II was 1978.

In 1979 ICL released a departmental mini called the ME29. This had as
part of the system an application that drove a Prestel service.
Televisions were being made, in the UK at least, with Teletext
decoders built in. Services such as CeeFax broadcast information in
the TV flyback delay time which could be displayed on screen. In fact
this is still being done.

A Prestel service used the Ceefax decoder in the TV but drove it from
a keyboard and dialup 1200/75 modem unit to a computer service. This
allowed the system to be interactive by entering queries or data and
getting responses back to your TV. As part of the support job for the
ME29 I developed some simple interactive demonstrations.
```

<a id="t-3d33d54831"></a>
## Thread: Mistah Dishwashah, He Dead... r' Jes' Sleepin'? — 16 msgs

### Mistah Dishwashah, He Dead... r' Jes' Sleepin'?

- **From:** docdwarf@panix.com ()
- **Date:** 2011-07-10T07:12:46+00:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ivbjde$nmp$1@reader1.panix.com>`

```

Subject: more-or-less says it all... a GSD500G-01AW, filled and powered-up 
the usual way... but something at the back of my neck, that low, 
murmuring, leftover-lizard part of the brain that deals with machinery 
('Whack it... *there*.' 'I said... what it... *thudthere*' 'chug chug chug 
chug chug'... 'how does he *do* that?)

... something at the back of my head said 'not tonight, Josephine... let's 
just watch the machinery work.' The pipe hummed... and water went in... 
and more water went in... and at the point where it should have gone 
*chunk* and the innards started spinning...

... nothing happened.  In a few moments foam decided the dishwasher was 
too crowded and should be exited; I turned the cycle-switch to the Drain 
point for that section and the water exited where it should have.

Being the kind of fellow I am I let everything sit for long enough so that 
one could have had a cigarette... and did the routine again.  Clik to 
fill, pipe, she go pppssssssshhhhh... and foam, speaking some rather gooey 
diphthongs, began to make its way to liberty again.

My guess is that DeForrest Kelley would have said 'I'm a doctor, not a 
physician... Jim, he's dead.'

But... tonight Mr Insomnia decided to stop by so I figured that I'd tell 
some folks a story.  Some folks, having particularly configured killfiles, 
would not know that happened, other folks, having other configurations, 
would skip on to the next thread... and still others might say 'Yep, 
r'minds me'a th' time that ol' Cousin Rutheford flooded out his 
mother-in-law's kitchen, newly remodelled in lime-green... if you thought 
they weren't the best of friends before, well, let me tell *you*...'

... and maybe... jest maybe... someone out there would say 'I've seen 
something like that, all ya gotta do is pop open the side panel and you'll 
see this little box labelled 'Gnihtyreve Seod' (they didn't want to make 
it *too* easy) and snag one off eBay... last time I got one it was three 
tuppence, a groat and since they'd just had Garlic Stew they threw in, for 
free, a great farthing... worked fine for another year.

'Rutherford did the same thing and it was DOA, had to start all over... 
hey, you got a bunch o' decades out of machine, it's not like it owes you 
all that much.'

DD
```

Alistair Maclean wrote:

Generally, I'd agree, but I recently had to replace a refrigerator and a 
stove and, although the modern ones are ... well, modern, there was an 
attraction about the old ones. I'm still getting used to a ceramic hob but 
the fan forced oven has impressed me :-)

I actually detected the problem with the fridge.  (It was a heating coil for 
defrosting which had slipped out of place) and I fixed it, but I had already 
decided to replace it so I gave it to a friend who needed a fridge. She 
tells me it is working perfectly (I had some reservations about the fix I 
made and told her so before she accepted it), so I guess some good came of 
it. It was pooling water under the vegetable bins. Turned out it was because 
a channel designed to vent water to the air had frozen up because the 
derfrosting coil had slipped...

The new one is all digital and smart and keeps things cool without frost 
forming on the beer. I can't fault it on looks or performance but I still 
liked the old one :-).

Sometimes, once you have decided to write something off (so it doesn't 
matter if you break it) it is fun to apply IT analysis processes and problem 
solution to something like a household appliance...

But generally, you can't be wrong if you get a new one :-)

Pete.
```

###### ↳ ↳ ↳ Re: Mistah Dishwashah, He Dead... r' Jes' Sleepin'?

- **From:** Robert Wessel <robertwessel2@yahoo.com>
- **Date:** 2011-07-10T11:14:39-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<7sij17ldl2kkttmb48f272vqerc5de49q2@4ax.com>`
- **References:** `<ivbjde$nmp$1@reader1.panix.com> <ee2f8ddc-008a-4f2d-82f5-7f4cea26ca3c@y24g2000yqb.googlegroups.com> <97tlfaF1imU1@mid.individual.net>`

```
On Mon, 11 Jul 2011 00:49:44 +1200, "Pete Dashwood"
<dashwood@removethis.enternet.co.nz> wrote:

>Alistair Maclean wrote:
>> On Jul 10, 8:12 am, docdw...@panix.com () wrote:
…[21 more quoted lines elided]…
>derfrosting coil had slipped...


The defroster coils (or associated thermostat) are probably the #1
cause of refrigerators failing.  If you see ice or frost on the back
(or sometimes sides) of the freezer compartment, that's almost always
the problem - the evaporator coils have frozen up, and are encased in
a nice insulating block of ice.

It's also a highly independent fault, and bad defrosters are basically
no indication of any other issues with the device.

On most refrigerators replacing the coils (typically there is more
than one coil, and they should *always* be replaced as a set) and
thermostat is pretty trivial, and well within the capability of anyone
semi-competent with a screwdriver.  Usually the service manuals are
easy to find (if only for ease of finding the part number, or there
are plenty of parts vendors on the Internet with decent search
functions, or if you live near a big city, there's usually several
decently stocked parts stores).  There is sometimes an exercise in
hidden screw removal, but it's usually not too bad.

Usually a new set of defroster coils is on the order of $50 (I
replaced a coil/thermostat set for $51 on a 17 year old GE fridge a
couple of months ago - oddly, that's the second time I've done it on
that fridge, the first was a couple of months after the warranty
expired).  Given the cost of new refrigerators, that's a reasonable
investment.  And once you defrost the evaporator coils, it'll work for
a week or two without issues (even without the defroster coils
working, it'll take that long to freeze up again), so you can spend a
few days farting around without too much hassle.

Of course when my mom's died last year (I'm pretty sure the defrosters
were only one of the problems, I know one of the fans was out too),
she decided she "needed" a stainless steel one.

What's all this got to do with Cobol?

## Thread: What are chances no Social Security checks? — 89 this month / 91 total

### What are chances no Social Security checks?

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-07-11T10:10:55-05:00
- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<WoCdna_ovpQcj4bTnZ2dnUVZ_rOdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
As a result of hitting the debt ceiling?

I suggest no chance inasmuch as most SS funds transfers are electronic. The 
program is wired to start sending out electronic funds transfers when the 
target date arrives. I'll bet there is no parameter that says: "Don't 
actually, you know, send the money."

If that's the case, in order to wedge such an option into the SS benefits 
program, weeks of programming and months of testing would be required.

'Course they COULD just turn off the mainframes, if, and this is a big if, 
there's anybody left alive who remembers where the ON/OFF switch IS. And all 
the redundant mainframes.
```

##### ↳ ↳ Re: What are chances no Social Security checks?

- **From:** "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
- **Date:** 2011-07-11T14:05:12-05:00

- **Message-ID:** `<ZaednVYzfPT01IbTnZ2dnUVZ_qqdnZ2d@earthlink.com>`

```
Alistair Maclean wrote:
> On Jul 11, 4:10 pm, "HeyBub" <hey...@NOSPAMgmail.com> wrote:
>> As a result of hitting the debt ceiling?
…[16 more quoted lines elided]…
> with the same rates as the last agreed budget?

No, it's a reference to the debt ceiling and the capacity of the U.S. to 
borrow.

You're thinking of appropriations. Often, when there is disagreement or a 
budget has not passed (which has happened for the last two years), Congress 
passes a "continuing resolution" to allow agencies to continue spending at 
previous levels.

The current contretemps has to do with whether there will be any money in 
the sock to spend with.
```

>ourselves.

The word "entitlement" is a funny one.   It used to mean all of those
things that the rich and powerful had, and they told us it was God's
will.    Are we entitled to life, liberty, and the pursuit of
happiness?   (Sure, as long as we the powerful don't have to pay for
them).

Now it is used to describe people getting what they paid for - which
they were forced to pay for.   And it's certainly not God's will
anymore (according to spokesmen for the Rich and Powerful).
```

- **Newsgroups:** comp.lang.cobol
- **Message-ID:** `<ivp40i$6mo$2@reader1.panix.com>`

```
In article <73tt179u8lp11vmtlo5ka6pp6puv3384ln@4ax.com>,
Howard Brazee  <howard@brazee.net> wrote:
>On Tue, 12 Jul 2011 07:29:03 -0500, "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com>
>wrote:
…[5 more quoted lines elided]…
>ancestors have earned.

Perhaps so, perhaps no, Mr Brazee.  I see it as 'the dead are entitled to 
have their wishes honored' since if one dies intestate (without a will) in 
most of the United States of America the status of the estate left behind 
is kind of murky... and *much* more generous to The State than anyone 
might realise.

Many (there are *so* many!) of the current inheritance tax laws are 
graduated, ie they subtract smaller-percentage from a smaller estate and 
larger-percentage from a larger estate.  (This mirrors the brackets of the 
Federal income tax, to an extent.)  What I've noticed is that the changes 
proposed to estate taxes seem to have the effect of maintaining a 
concentration of wealth as dictated by the deceased.

Is that a Good Thing?  I have no idea; I do not recall the last time when 
the concentration of wealth in the United States of America was what it is 
nowadays and it might be so long ago as to render comparison meaningless.

(folks argue, at times, as though this sort of stuff is easy... 'See, All 
Ya Gotta Do is...')

DD
```

```
HansJ wrote:
> On 12 Jul., 12:07, Alistair Maclean <alistair.j.l.macl...@gmail.com>
> wrote:
…[31 more quoted lines elided]…
>> them out.

A quick look at history shows that the alternative to working together in 
Europe is war in Europe.

The EU arose, not just from a common sense realization that economic 
prosperity encourages peace, but also  from the fact that the effects of two 
world wars were fresh in everyone's mind and the horrific cost of these 
conflicts was not something that could be accepted for a third time. Better 
to waste money, have a mountain of butter and a lake of wine, than to waste 
lives and have a mountain of bodies and a lake of blood.

(I'd also like to think that lessons were learned and we grew as peoples, 
but I can't prove that.)

>
> Well, I'm affraid there is some truth to it. Just spending money in
> order to keep those states in a condition that they can pay their
> bills is not good enough, they need to get their spending habits
> right, just like us.

Sure. I believe the IMF (when they're not trying to have sex with chamber 
maids) place conditions on the bail out loans which are designed to enforce 
better money management. It is a long process.

>
> The EU should restructure rather than thinking about getting more
> members.
>
> ... how does this relate to COBOL?

I changed the topic. Now it doesn't have to... :-)

For years now it would seem that the most interesting discussions here are 
not necessarily about COBOL. Just because the language is dying doesn't mean 
the Newsgroup has to.  Personally, I do like to work some COBOL connection 
into my posts but I don't always succeed.

I'm not sure that it really matters, Hans.

People who only come here for COBOL can ignore posts clearly marked OT; the 
rest can read whatever's posted (or not :-)) and enjoy it. (Or not :-)). The 
secret is to clearly mark topics and that doesn't always happen, 
unfortunately.

This is an unmoderated forum where people can say anything they like. As 
such it is a bastion of free speech and we should treasure it. Several 
people have suggested I run a blog (for various reasons) but I know that a 
good blog requires a huge amount of effort and time and I don't always have 
those available. An unmoderated forum is ideal because if I have time (and 
inclination) I can post, and if I don't have time, I don't need to worry 
about the blog dying due to neglect. Besides, I actually enjoy many of the 
posts here so, as long as I have time, I'm happy to be here.

The days when people came here looking for advice on COBOL are pretty much 
gone, and I get more private COBOL related enquiries than are posted here. I 
always tell them to post publicly so other people can benefit but they don't 
seem to want to. Even so, the number of enquiries I receive privately has 
fallen significantly in the last two years. Now it's down to about one every 
7 weeks or so, and they are usually pertaining to Power COBOL which is a 
specialised topic. Most of these requests come from South America, where 
COBOL  (certainly PowerCOBOL) seems to be alive and well.  I have also had a 
few from Europe (Germany and Italy, mainly).

I am amazed that people are still using it but they are, even though Fujitsu 
(Alchemy) apparently are not offering support for it any more.

I'm currently writing some PowerCOBOL for a client who needs to interface to 
some odd devices using Windows sockets across the LAN. The actual socket 
manager I wrote in C# because .Net sockets Class is just so much simpler 
than the Windows API and the Windows Sockets .DLL. It can use delegation for 
multi threading of asynchronous requests and I'm not sure how to do that in 
PowerCOBOL... :-)

It's a bit of a horror show from a development point of view because the 
PowerCOBOL is written using the Fujitsu IDE and the C# is written using VS 
2010. I have the PowerCOBOL on a virtual machine and yesterday I 
accidentally disconnected the external drive it resides on. Lost about 3 
hours work, to my chagrin. VS 2010 saves every 5 minutes... the COBOL 
IDE...never (unless you do it manually). I need to have two copies of the 
COBOL open (one is read only) so I can check datanames and pictures. In C#, 
Intellisense does it all for you without any need for a separate reference 
copy. I have become aware of just how primitive the PowerCOBOL IDE really is 
and yet I used it for years and thought it was good.

I had a look at writing the Sockets Manager in PowerCOBOL to keep everything 
consistent but it would have been several hundred lines to implement socket 
creation and connection, non-blocking receive with callback, static send, 
and managing the protocol infrastructure of ACKs and NAKs etc so the 
application sees only messages. In C# it is a few dozen lines.

(There, Hans, COBOL was discussed... :-))

Pete.
```

The amount of COBOL used by the federal Government would astonish
most people, especially all the say-sayer who think COBOL is dead.
One of the largest IT systems in the world is the IRS and it is
Unisys COBOL (with a littel assembler thrown in just for fun!)  The
DOD hospital system, that is all the Army, Navy, Marine Corps and Air
Force hospitals, medical and dental clinics and much more is all done
in COBOL on IBM mainframes.  They recently put out an announcement
for an internship program targeted at 1st year CS students to work
for them in the summer learning COBOL and have a nice job waiting for
them when they graduate.  The schools have stopped teaching it but
the need has anything but gone away and now these places wil have to
resort to rolling thier own!!

And I am sure there many, many more very large COBOL IT systems that
just are not going to go away!!  Sorry to disappoint you, Pete.  :-)

bill

```
Bill Gunshannon wrote:
> In article <AsGdnYLdxeWEPIHTnZ2dnUVZ_o6dnZ2d@earthlink.com>,
> "HeyBub" <heybub@NOSPAMgmail.com> writes:
…[20 more quoted lines elided]…
>> a patch over one eye. And a limp.

LOL! You met some of my team... :-)
>
> The amount of COBOL used by the federal Government would astonish
…[13 more quoted lines elided]…
> just are not going to go away!!  Sorry to disappoint you, Pete.  :-)

I could respond to this but there is no point really. I happen to know that 
the USAF is moving off COBOL internally (maybe not for their medical systems 
but certainly for other admin) . As for the IRS, the worse their systems 
are, the better for everybody :-) It's silly to argue this because for every 
major installation you can quote, I can quote one that has gone off COBOL. 
What does it achieve? If you choose to believe that COBOL is alive and well, 
good for you. As someone who made a living from it for decades, I know that 
simply isn't the case and it only takes a search on job sites like SEEK and 
JobServe to see that COBOL jobs are a very small percentage of the IT scene. 
(20 years ago they were an overwhelming percentage. When something goes from 
preponderance to insgnificance, it is fair to say it is in "decline"...) 
Nevertheless, you are right that it isn't dead yet and some places will 
continue using it for some time.

Just because I call it as I see it does NOT mean I am pleased about it.

As for being disappointed, my primary concern was some years back when I 
realized that all my own COBOL code was going nowhere, and it looked like 
the vendor may either cease to exist or stop supporting it. (They 
subsequently divested themselves of it and today the new owners only support 
a part of the product range. I developed enough expertise in the area to be 
able to support it myself, and now other people ask me for help....Wherever 
I can I give it freely.) As I have now migrated, I no longer have that 
concern and I spend a lot of time helping other people achieve the same 
peace of mind. :-) I do encourage people to move off COBOL but it isn't 
because I hate the language (I actually don't...); it is the old story about 
giving a man a fish or a fishing line.

If COBOL was the right tool for developing networked systems in the 21st 
century, I'd be waving the banner for it (I actually loved writing COBOL and 
I think most people who write it feel that way), but it isn't.

I need objects and layers. COBOL is not good at that. Goodbye COBOL.

Pete.
```

those systems that are still in production for some time.

for Unisys mainframes is fairly small.

especially if the platforms used are not found in many IT shops.

supply, though it might have a different price tag in the future.

In article <fd238e7c-00dc-44b4-8943-d342981056d9@fv14g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>,
	HansJ <hans.igel@googlemail.com> writes:
> On 14 Jul., 23:47, billg...@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon) wrote:
>> In article <25440aa2-d61d-4606-ac1e-d55427d0f...@eb1g2000vbb.googlegroups.com>,
…[53 more quoted lines elided]…
> before I've to check for the features of a casket :-(

And you think it is somehow different outside of government hiring?
A lot of the government is using Resumix, as is a lot of the commercial world.
They also use some package from Monster as do a lot of the commercial world.
In every instance that I am aware of the first scan is little more than
a keyword scan done by machine.  If you are lucky enough to get by this
a human who actually know what some of it means is next.  But even with
30 years of experience I have had little luck getting a resume through
step one at most agencies.  I have applied for jobs at DISA that were
for the exact work I was doing for DISA in 2009 when I was mobilized
only to get bumped at the very first level with "does not meet minimum
requirements".

The really sad part of this is that most government (and most commercial)
hiring has become a resume writing contest and does not in any way reflect
an ability to "hire the very best" as is claimed on most government HR
sites.  One has to be unbelievably tenacious to actually get considered
for a job.

bill

>"2210" as the Series Number.  That's IT.

Mr Wiegand, as Mr Gushannon pointed out these are government jobs; the 
benefits are government benefits and I think one might even enjoy the 
protection of a union (*gasp*).  It is my experience, however, that such 
high-grade positions are (more often than not) more managerial and less 
technical.

(Also, in my experience, these positions - a GS-13 gets a side-wall office 
with a door which closes and a GS-14 occupies a corner office - are often 
*grossly* politicised; people spend decades working towards them and a 
posting is usually a bit of shadow-theatre.  As was sung in Gilbert & 
Sullivan's 'The Mikado':

'If you think we are worked by strings,
Like a Japanese marionette,
You don't understand these things,
It is simply court etiquette.')

DD
```

> Bill Gunshannon <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote:

> posting is usually a bit of shadow-theatre.  

Which would make sense except these jobs were posted to all US Citizens,
no prior government service required.  You can find similar jobs with
even GS-15 in the mix with organizations like DISA.  The offer is almost
always dependant on the skill-set needed.  These are UNISYS COBOL jobs.
I don't imagine there are a lot of experienced UNISYS COBOL Programmers
in the job search market.  The DISA jobs are IT security related and
tend to require things like lots of DISA training and CISSP.  The pool
of DISA trained people is rather limited but the CISSP is highly in demand
outside the government making it necessary to offer competitive salaries.

	"Charles Hottel" <chottel@earthlink.net> writes:
> 
> "Bill Gunshannon" <billg999@cs.uofs.edu> wrote in message 
…[45 more quoted lines elided]…
> better qualified than outside applicants. 
 
Why would they bother doing that?

WHO MAY BE CONSIDERED: IRS employees on Career/Career Conditionali
                       appointments in the competitive service
                       Treasury Office of Chief Counsel employees on
                       Career or Career Conditional Appointment or with
                       prior competitive status
                       Treasury Inspector General of Tax Administration
                       (TIGTA) employees on Career or Career Conditional
                       Appointments or with prior competitive status

This limits applicants to in-house.

And if you looked at the bottom of the search page you would have seen:

Are you a current or former Federal civilian employee who holds or heldr
 a non-temporary appointment?:
          In the competitive service in the Executive Branch or in a
          position not in the Executive branch specifically included
          in the competitive service by statute, or 
          In an excepted service position covered by an interchange
          agreement , or
          Eligible for reinstatement ? 
          A Veteran eligible for veterans' preference or separated from
          the armed forces under honorable conditions after 3 years or
          more of continuous military service? 
          A person with non-competitive appointment eligibility?

Which separates people with some factor which gives them preference in
government hiring from the general public.

One need not play games with qualifications.  The IRS advertised (and still
does periodically) for COBOL programmers strictly in-house.  I would imagine
that ppol is rapidly drying up and thus the need to widen their search to
the general public.

bill

"SkippyPB" <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message 
news:072627te152rsgd4bvi0u5dku08lemaked@4ax.com...
> On 16 Jul 2011 23:45:23 GMT, billg999@cs.uofs.edu (Bill Gunshannon)
> wrote:
…[59 more quoted lines elided]…
>
<snip>

Not all agencies have unions.  Mine does but  joining is optional.  If you 
do not join and pay dues you probably will get less direct union support but 
you will still benefit from whatever the union manages to negotiate.  After 
a certain GS level you become management an are not able to join the union.

I worked at a GSA telework center from 1998 until October 2010.  Since that 
time I have worked from my home.  Telework is being promoted more and the 
union is negotiating a general telework policy for allt, but there is still 
a lot of managerial resistance.  Colocation of employees is often considerd 
necessary and also a reason to block telework.  My experience with being 
able to telework so much is definitely atypical from what most people 
experience.  Off and on I was required to go to the office one day a week. 
At other times they needed more space and I did not go into the office.
```

```

"SkippyPB" <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message 
news:u0c327prm3q5qeiuom0u0d11bdvf6iq4ki@4ax.com...
> On Fri, 15 Jul 2011 21:23:31 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
> <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
…[75 more quoted lines elided]…
>
Some benefits come with the salary.  The government pays part (1/2 ?) of 
your health insurance costs and you pay the rest, but you pay with untaxed 
money.  The basic health insurance covers some, but not much, of vision and 
dental expenses.  You can get additional vision and dental but you pay for 
that.  The vision insurance is pretty cheap but the dental is expensive. You 
contribute to the FERS retirement system but you can receive some government 
matching.  In addition to FERS you can put money into the Thrift Savings 
Plan (TSP) which is similar to a 401(K) plan. The money that goes into the 
TSP is untaxed money. You pay federal and state taxes, and social security 
and medicare taxes. You can put money into a Health Care and or a Child Care 
Flexible Spending account (FSA).  Money going into the FSA is untaxed money. 
There are life insurance option that you can select and you pay for them.

I think it is a pretty good deal when you consider the retirement benefits. 
As good as it seems to me, I believe that most of the IT contractors at my 
agency are making more money than than goverment employees.  In my career it 
was pretty easy to advance to the GS 13 level, but vacancies at GS 14 and 
above are fairly rare, at least in my agency.  There have only been about 
four that I applied for since the mid 1990's.
```

On Sun, 17 Jul 2011 11:00:54 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
<chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:

>
>"SkippyPB" <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message 
…[98 more quoted lines elided]…
>

The health, dental and vision insurance you mention is not much
different than what I get in my private sector job.  Dental is high
and doesn't provide very much, vision is cheap and health isn't too
bad depending on what you select.  We usually have 9 or 10 plans to
choose from every year.  We also have FSA if you wish it.  Also, like
the government, untaxed money.  We don't have FERS or TSP but we do
have 401K with company matching funds up to a point.

Even in the private sector you run up against wage scale maximums and
the ability to go up to the next level.  In my case the next level is
mid management and having been there and done that and not real happy
with it, don't want to do that again.  The downside of course is I've
nearly maxed out my level in terms of salary so I may just have to
make what I make until retirement.  There are worse things.
Unemployment is worse.

Regards,

"SkippyPB" <swiegand@Nospam.neo.rr.com> wrote in message 
news:in2627pbe1q7sbj099vvop8ue88t6n7vpl@4ax.com...
> On Sun, 17 Jul 2011 11:00:54 -0400, "Charles Hottel"
> <chottel@earthlink.net> wrote:
…[129 more quoted lines elided]…
